\lx čʰə·šašiw
\ps n
\ge (at the, on the) dog
\ge placename--near Terminus 
\sf media\dog {loc}.mp3
\nt Dog place is place near Terminus that was scary because doctors stored their sacred belongings there and protected them with spells that could make you sick. 
\nd Listen to Cecile's account of this scary place:
\xv 0. čʰə·šašiw 1. hiyam'u p'axintʰay tʰaw ṭʰaʔaṭʰi 2. ʔaŋṭʰaw hintʰaw ʔot'oy ʔanik' mononhay xoʔo·xiʔ pʰata·w'iw 3. ʔamaʔ hawtaw watʰtʰaw tʰanaʔat tʰaŋ ŋeʔwaw 4. ʔamaʔ čʰə·šaš tʰašti poy'um'hay kəwtat čaʔaʔpa tʰaŋ to·xoxintʰa 5. yow čaʔaʔpa ʔam tušaʔa tʰaŋ č'aʔapa ʔam tʰihin' tʰaŋ p'axa·nat 6. ʔamaʔ hawtaw tʰaw watxutʰa tʰoyon'ow yow we·ʔi ŋeʔwaw ṭʰipʰin ʔan tomtʰow 7. ʔamaʔ tʰašti hiṭ'wa·yoʔ yawa·tit tʰaw tʰihin' ʔam to·čoxin 8. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' ʔanik' xew pʰitaw ŋattʰaw haynat 9.  ʔamaʔ yič'ŋa watxutʰa tʰa ʔanik' p'axin ʔamaʔ ʔam tʰašti ṭʰukʰo·ʔat 10. yow hičʰa tʰihin' to·čoxintʰa ʔamaʔ tʰašti čʰɨxa·čʰantʰa 11. ʔamaʔ xoʔ ʔanik' tʰaw ʔaŋṭʰawhin mononhay ṭ'iʔiw hantʰaw 12. yow tʰaw xo·ʔoxiʔ may' ʔanik' toʔoṭ'a ʔanik' ʔot'oy 13. yow ʔanik' čʰɨxčʰɨn tʰaw xo·ʔoxiʔ k'umuy hantʰaw ʔam tʰaŋ hun'ay'may' ʔɨk'a·ʔat 14. ʔamaʔ čʰan ʔaŋṭʰawhin tʰa č'o·w'oŋiṭʰ
\xe 0. Dog place 1. Long ago the people there were scared. 2. The doctors around there put their belongings in the cave. 3. And when whoever goes by it in the evening, 4. Than all the pretty dogs meet them -- some of them get scared. 5. And some do not -- they were told about it by some others and the are not scared by it. 6. And when they pass by there above its hill at night or sometimes in the evening, 7. Then the evil spirit follows them if they don't get scared. 8. Then they jump off tho the side of their road. 9. And their fear passes by them alone and it doesn't catch them. 10. Or if they get scared then it makes them sick. 11. And that is where the doctors' belongings and whatever else is. 12. And that is where they put their human heads. 13. And their sickness is there also, all those things a common person can not see. 14. It is just the doctors' working things.
\sfx media\dog place story.mp3
\u čʰə·šaš/ -w
\mr locative case
\dt 08/Nov/2018

\lx č'omtat
\ps v
\ge washing face in face washing ceremony
\sf media\face washing {d-pres}.mp3
\nt Here is Cecile's account of face washing as part of the mourning ceremony:
\xv 1. hawtaw ʔanik' hantʰaw tʰawaṭʰat 2. ʔamaʔ tʰihinʔ ponxat hawtaw tʰihin' hunŋiṭʰ wita 3. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' kɨʔya ʔanik' k'ə·xaŋ wak'yat 4. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' ʔot'yit ʔopʰoʔtoʔ hawtaw tʰihin' tuyo·na 5. ʔamaʔ ʔanik' kɨʔɨy tʰašti č'omtat 6. k'ə·xaŋ tʰihin' wak'yat yow kɨʔya ʔanik' 7. ʔamaʔ ʔanik' kɨʔɨy tʰašti č'omtat wak'yat tʰašti 8. hawšun tʰihin' ʔam to·yatiy tʰašti k'umuy č'omtat 9. ʔamaʔ tʰašti wak'yat mononhayŋi yow k'ə·xaŋ 10. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' piyʔin ʔanik' č'umuthan ṭʰe·naṭʰintʰin 11. čʰa tʰihin' tuytat ʔanik' kɨʔya yow tʰihin' may'a čʰan tuyo·na še·šatʰ
\xe 1. When someone has died, 2. then they fasted until the time (end of mourning) came. 3. Then they give their face washer money. 4. Then they pick a day when they will eat, 5.  and their face washer washed them. 6. Then give their face washer money again, 7. and their face washer gives them their face washing. 8. All of those who have fasted get washed. 9. Then the face washer gives them clothes and money. 10. When they finish their face washing, lunch will be eaten. 11. Soon they feed the people, their face wahers, and soon they themselves eat meat.
\sfx media\Face washing story.mp3
\u #č'umut -at
\mr durative present
\dt 08/Apr/2018

\nt Cecile explains how to prepare white roots to use in making a basket:
\xv 0. čʰotot ho·pʰut 1. hopʰtat maʔ tʰaŋ ʔamaʔ maʔ tʰaŋ hopʰutʔun wɨxe·niʔin ṭ'oto·ʔot 2. ʔamaʔ k'umuy ṭ'otʔin wat'a·xat 3. ʔama tʰaŋ witʔin šiymanutʰa 4. šiyamunšiw ʔan ʔamaʔ tʰaŋ šototʰtat 5. ʔamaʔ tʰaŋ ṭ'ik'e·na ʔamaʔ tʰaŋ ʔot'yit hitew maʔ tʰaŋ ʔot'oyčʰinat 6. hawtaw maʔ tixatčʰinat ʔamaʔ tʰaŋ ṭ'ɨwan'a ʔamaʔ maʔ tʰaŋ hə·pʰɨt hawit maʔ ʔoho·ʔot tixta min tʰa·ŋi
\xe 0. White roots 1. When you dig white roots and you get a bunch of them, you peel them. 2. When all are peeled, you split them. 3. Then right away you let them dry. 4. After they dry then coil them up. 5. Then tie then (in a bundle) and put it wherever you want to put it. 6. When you want to make a basket, saok (the white roots) and then scrape however much you want to make your basket.
\sfx media\Preparing white roots.mp3

\lx huhunaʔ
\ps n
\ge a dance character who would find and keep your money
\sf media\dance character.mp3
\nt Here is Cecile's account of the time her father saw the Huhuna:
\xv 0. huhunaʔ 1. hiya·m'u wičʰp'ay ʔan natʰe·tʰin nim ʔə·k'aš ʔanik' toʔan 2. ʔamaʔ tʰašti teyaw ʔan tʰaxna tʰa ʔamaʔ tʰaŋ tʰašti xa·yaxiy k'ə·xɨyɨn hite·tʰaw ʔot'oy woŋ 3. ʔamaʔ hiyamxaš nihiʔ tʰaxin 4. ʔamaʔ naʔ puk'e·na tʰaŋ hiʔ 5. ʔamaʔ ye·t'aw k'aw ʔopʰoʔtoʔ tʰaxinši 6. ʔamaʔ tʰaŋ wuṭʰsi ʔan šitʰitčiŋ ʔan mononhay k'al'wittʰa 7. ʔamaʔ witʔin hu··hu··wittʰa tʰa·nitʰ tʰaw hewtʰat 8. ʔamaʔ tʰaŋ ʔə·k'aš hutšu tʰa·ŋi hitew xoʔ tʰa k'ə·xa 9. ʔamaʔ tʰaw tʰanši ʔamaʔ ʔan šitʰitčiŋ tʰaŋ tʰištaš puk'ši tʰaŋ k'ə·xa 10. wɨxnat witʰep nim n'ahi yit'an tʰaŋ ʔamaʔ čʰan ʔə·k'aš
\xe 0. huhuna 1. Long ago when my father was young, he saw them [huhuna] playing. 2. And before [huhuna] came there, they were all saying "Hide, put your money wherever." 3. And, I [huhuna] will come back tomorrow. 4. And I will find it. 5. And he came to the group the next day. 6. And he hit his cane on his clothes making the sound of kal. 7. And making the noise huuhuu walking away from there. 8. And he saw or knew where the money was hidden. 9. And he went there and with his cane took out and found the money. 10. My dad was very young and only saw it once.
\sfx media\Huhuna story - complete.mp3
\dt 01/Apr/2018

\lx huwčʰuwiš
\ps n
\ge dice game
\sf media\play dice {r-r}.mp3
\nt Here is Cecile's account of the dice game:
\xv 0. huwčʰuwiš 1. təʔsat tʰihin' huwočʰ huwo·čʰaŋ 2. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ k'umuy way'kʰat ʔan-nim k'ɨmay'aʔ 3. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ šipešpat čʰototya·tʰa 4. yakʰawaw tʰihin' tʰaŋ xɨp'xɨp'wittʰa 5. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ piyin ʔamaʔ tʰihin tʰaŋ ma·p'amip' ša·xalŋi yow we·ʔi ha·ŋitʰaw k'iw'šaŋ 6. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ ʔot'yit ma·p'amip' tʰihin' tʰaŋ teyaw ʔan č'ič'e·k'aŋṭʰi pʰo·tay 7. yow we·ʔi ṭʰuṭʰutŋihat tʰaŋ tʰihin' walamwitʰan ʔanik' t'im'ši 8. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' xoʔ ʔanik' k'ač'aw tɨʔasan'a tuʔo·huy ʔanik' hoyo·ʔoš ʔan kʰay'o·ṭʰuʔ 9. ʔamaʔ tʰaw tʰihin' tʰaŋ to·ʔatiʔ nay milikʰaniʔin toʔon 10. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' mun'oš ʔanik' huwočʰ 11. ʔamaʔ ʔanik' wič'etʰ we·ʔi ṭ'iy'ew yow poŋoy tɨʔasan'a wič'e·tʰaŋ 12. ʔam wɨxnat wəʔə·haṭ' 13. ʔamaʔ tʰa ʔanik' pʰič'e·hiy ʔanik' hawšun tʰihin' ṭ'a·w'aṭ'iw' 14. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' to·ʔatiʔ huwučʰwišat 15. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ t'e·m'at'im' 16. ʔamaʔ hawšun ʔattin xo·ʔoxiʔ yow hawšun ṭʰipʰnin 17. ʔamaʔ tʰa pʰič'an xo·ʔoxiʔ hawšun tʰaw ʔan pʰič' 18. yow hičʰa hatʰpaŋi ʔatit yow hatʰpaŋi ṭʰipʰin ʔamaʔ tʰaʔin tʰa xoʔ pʰič' 19. hawšun hičʰa k'umuy ṭ'ipʰnin xo·ʔoxiʔ ʔamaʔ tʰa hay'awšit tʰaʔin tʰaŋ hawšun tʰaw pʰič' 20. yow ʔattin k'umuy ṭɨmɨxwittʰa ʔamaʔ tʰaʔan tʰaʔan pʰič' xo·ʔoxiʔ 21. we·ʔi mɨŋɨŋši naʔ pʰič' hawšun ʔanik' pʰič' 22. ʔam naʔ tʰaŋ wɨxnat toʔo·šu 23. ʔamaʔ hawtaw tʰaw wa·tʰ ṭ'a·w'aṭʰ'iw' k'umuy tʰaŋ wič'etʰ ʔanik' ʔamaʔ tʰa ṭ'aw'a·ʔač' 24. xo·now tʰihin' to·ʔatiʔ muk'ešhi 25. yow muk'išhin toʔan ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ to·ʔatiʔ
\xe 0. Dice Game 1. They make dice with black walnut. 2. And they clean out all of its-my nut. 3. And they scrape it making it white. 4. They rub it repeatedly on a rock. 5. And when they finish it there, they fill it with tar or whatever hard thing they have. 6. And they put beads in, filling it before it cools. 7. or anything like abalone shells that shine when they are thrown 8. And they have their playing basket made, it is called kayowtu. 9. And they play on it like the white person's game. 10. And they have eight dice. 11. And they have their sticks or anything, twelve of them made from sticks 12. They are not too tall. 13. And they have these to count, however many points the are getting. 14. And they play the dice game. 15. And they throw them. 16. And however many are down or however many are up. 17. And that is it's count, however many is there is the count. 18. And if there are four down or four up then that is it's count. 19. However many, if they are all up then it is called laughing and that is how many counts there. 20. Or if they are all down it is called black and it has its count also. 21. I forgot anything on the count, how many of them count. 22. I didn't play it very much. 23. And when someone wins, they win all their sticks then they are the winner. 24. They were always playing, the women. 25. And it was a women's game and they played it.
\sfx media\Dice Game story.mp3
\pc media\Dice Game.jpg
\nt Picture from the Smithsonian Institution photo archives -- mostly likely from F. Latta 1944.
\u huwu·čʰ/ -wiš
\mr reflexive-reciprocal
\nt Related languages Yawelamni huwṭuwus  Chukchansi huwčuwṣ-an
\dt 04/Jul/2018

\lx huʔtʰutu/

\dt 28/Sep/2017

\lx huʔtʰutuʔ
\ps n
\ge owl
\sf media\owl.mp3
\xv ʔamaʔ tʰihik' taŋši huʔtʰutuʔ ʔan taŋta
\xe Then they heard an owl hooting.
\sfx media\Then they heard an owl hooting.mp3
\nt There are several stories involving Owl. Cecile's version of The Man and the Owl is a good example:
\xv 1. yet' waṭʰač'i kʰuhṭ'un ʔamaʔ ʔan yow yiw'in ʔamaʔ tʰihik' pʰaʔṭʰawtʰawši  2. ʔamaʔ tʰaški tʰuyin'ši ʔamaʔ tʰihik' taka·ši pʰata·w'iw ʔamaʔ tʰihik' hot'o·ʔoniš 3. ʔamaʔ tʰihik' taŋši huʔtʰutuʔ ʔan taŋta 4. ʔamaʔ muk'iš tʰa xa·yaxiy yiw'enč'i ʔan hawtin maʔan tʰašti ho·yihiy naʔan hutaw hoyhiya ʔamaʔ hiʔ tʰaxin ʔamaʔ tʰaŋ hiʔ t'uyo·na ʔamaʔ mak' tʰaŋ hiʔ tuyo·na taŋtʰuman 5. ʔamaʔ yiw'e·nič' maxši ʔan tatpʰa ʔamaʔ yow sikʰtʰa ʔamaʔ tʰašti hoyo·ši 6. ʔamaʔ huʔtʰutuʔ pʰučši ʔamaʔ tʰaŋ yow hoyo·ši ʔamaʔ yow hoyo·ši ʔamaʔ tʰaw huʔtʰutuʔ tʰaŋ pʰučši  7. ʔamaʔ ʔatit ʔattin tʰašti ʔamaʔ xo·ʔoxiʔ paʔṭʰawtin tʰašti ʔac'ew ʔankin ʔošto  8. ʔamaʔ tʰaŋ yiw'e·nič' ʔan t'uyšu tʰawtʰaš tʰaŋ 9. ʔamaʔ ʔan yiw'in tošši tʰaŋ yow wuy wittʰa tʰaŋ ʔamaʔ yow yet' tʰaxinšiya 10.  ʔamaʔ yow hoyo·ši ʔamaʔ yow yet' yow tʰaxinši ʔamaʔ tʰaŋ yow t'uyšu 11. ʔamaʔ han hiye·t'it tʰa xa·xiy hiyam pʰaw'ši wita 12. ʔamaʔ ʔan yiw'in k'amowittʰa yow tʰašti hoyoʔ
\xe  1. One waksachi man and his wife were fighting with one another. 2. And it got dark on them and they stayed overnight in a cave and they built a fire. 3. Then they heard an owl hooting. 4. Then the woman said to her husband: why don't you call them all the same way and and they will come and we can shoot them and eat them for supper. 5. The husband got his bow and his arrows then he called them all. 6. Then the owl answered him and he called again then the owl answered him again. 7. Then it sat down near the fighting ones' fire. 8. And her husband shot it. 9. Then his wife told him: do it again and another will come. 10. Again he called and again it came and again he shot it. 11. Then he was happy with that and said: that is enough now. 12. But his wife said no, call them again!
\sfx media\Man and Owl 1.mp3
\xv 13. tʰaw mihiʔ tʰaŋ hoyoʔ šal'al'witaw ʔam hiʔ tʰaxin 14. ʔam mak' hiyam'a še·šat to·yatyaš ʔoho·čʰinat hin xo·ʔoxiʔ tuy ʔamaʔ yow hiyamxaš 15. ʔamaʔ tʰašti yow hoyo·ši tʰašti yow tʰaxinši huʔtʰutuʔ 16. čʰan ʔač'inʔiy xo·ʔotʰ yow tʰašti t'uyšu 17. ʔamaʔ yow tʰaxnat 18. hiyam tʰihin' wɨxe·ʔat 19. hiyam ʔan t'uyinši tʰatupʰ 20.  hiyam ʔam puk'etʰši ʔinšiš 21. ʔamaʔ puk'pok' tʰaxinši ʔač'ew ʔamaʔ tʰasti šiwšiwši 22. ʔamaʔ sunsun tʰaŋ tʰanʔašiš ʔoštʰow ʔan wič'etʰaw 23. ʔamaʔ tʰašti tʰa·ŋi witʔin tʰa·wiṭʰši 24. ʔamaʔ ʔan yiw'na k'ač'awaŋ ṭʰumši 25. ʔamaʔ yow tʰaxinši huʔtʰutuʔ šipešpiʔiy ʔamaʔ tʰasti k'umuy šuxšu
\xe 13. If you call them in the morning, they will not come. 14. You and I have not eaten meat for a long time, won't we want some food for tomorrow? 15. Then he called again and again an owl came. 16. Soon they were near and he shot them all. 17. And they were coming again. 18. Already there were many. 19. He could not shoot his bow already. 20. It was not good being found already. 21. And finding them they owls came near and they scratched the couple. 22. Then they were prohibiting the couple from going, so he got a burning stick. 23. And he killed them with it right away. 24. Then he covered his wife with a basket. 25. The the owl came again and as a result of the scratching the couple were killed.
\sfx media\Man and Owl 2.mp3

\lx kiṭ'inʔiy
\ps n
\sn 1
\ge something to be carried under the arm
\sn 2
\ge mountain balm
\sf media\mountain balm.mp3
\pc media\Ceanothus velutinus.jpg
\nt picture from en.hortipedia.com
\sc Mountain Balm (Sticky Laurel, Tobacco Brush, Snowbrush, Buckbush) Ceanothus velutinus
\u #kiṭ'e·n -ʔiy
\mr consequent adjunctive
\nt leaves were used as a deodorant and steeped in water to make a tea used as a medicine for stomach ailments and colds. In strong concentrations it is a diuretic. Here is Cecile's discussion of Mountain Balm: 
\xv 1. kiṭ'inʔiy ʔinšiš tʰaŋ t'oyox 2. ho·pʰut ʔan tʰaʔan ʔinšiš hə·piʔin 3. ʔamaʔ ʔan t'ap't'ap' tʰiʔanʔin tʰaʔan ʔohoyo 4. hiya·m'u hiyamxay keṭ'nat ʔinšiš tumukʰnaw k'amona·tʰa k'awk'awṭʰiʔin hitew tʰanši 5. ʔamaʔ witʔin hoyotʰ kiṭ'inʔiy
\xe 1. Mountain Balm is good medicine 2. Its roots are good for the blood 3. and its leaves make tea for colds 4. Long ago, old people put it under their arm for sweating, it takes away the armpit smell 5. and that is why they call it kiṭ'inʔiy now.
\sfx media\Mountain Balm story.mp3

\lx kʰoxo·mik'
\ps n

\ge Arrowroot
\sf media\potato-like plant {nom}.mp3
\pc media\Sagittaria_latifolia_(flowers).jpg
\nt picture from en.wikipedia.org
\sc Sagittaria latifolia
\nt Cecile talks about another potato-like food. The tubers from this plant were also cooked and eaten:
\xv 1. yet' yow xo·ʔoxiʔ tʰaʔan ʔanik' wɨxnat ʔinšin 2. hoyo·ši ʔan kʰoxo·mik' 3. pa·pas nay ʔan xo·ʔoxiy ʔam čʰan'iʔ meṭʰnay puninhat tʰaŋ tʰihin' tʰaʔan ʔotʰtat 4. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ witʔin yuk'onon 5. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ šo·t'ašit' 6. šut'un tʰihin' tʰaŋ tʰaʔan ʔamaʔ tʰihin' šičʰe·niʔin to·yatiy hokʰyat ʔanik tuya
\xe 1. There is one other of their plants that is also good. 2. It was called kohomik. 3. It is potato like and not very big, small things that they also dig. 4. And they will wash it right away. 5. Then they boil it. 6. They will also boil it and when it is cool they eat it mixed with their other food.
\sfx media\Arrowroot story.mp3
\dt 22/Feb/2019

\lx k'am'a·yaʔ
\ps n
\ge Mariposa Lily
\sf media\mariposa lily.mp3
\pc media\Mariposa lily combined.jpg
\nt Pictures by Beatrice F. Howitt, California Academy of Sciences (1999) and sustainablelivingproject.blogspot.com
\sc Calochortus venustus
\nt The bulbs of the Mariposa Lily were a favorite food. Here is Cecile's account of collecting and preparing them:
\xv 1. k'am'a·yaʔ 2. hiya·m'u tʰihin' k'umuy ha·ʔatʰaw maxa·ʔat ʔinya·niʔin tuya 3. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' k'am'a·yaʔ ʔotʰtat 4. ʔutʰit tʰihin' tʰaŋ ʔanik' šitʰitčiŋ 5. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' ʔotʰitʔin wɨxi 6. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ ṭʰe·ʔiw tʰantiʔin ʔot'yit tʰihin' ʔanik' ʔapa·tiw 7. ʔamaʔ tʰihinʔ tʰaŋ xuša·ʔat k'umuy ʔan tʰišta·tʰa tiŋta yow ʔan ṭ'əʔčʰa 8. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ witʔin šo·t'ašit' 9. tʰaw tʰihin' hičʰa tʰumatan'a ʔoho·ʔot ʔamaʔ tʰihin' ʔam ṭ'otʔin tʰaŋ 10. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' hapasaw yow tok'tat 11.  ʔamaʔ tʰa·nitʰ se·sasis haptunutʰ 12. ʔamaʔ hawtaw se·sasis ʔamaʔ tʰihin' ʔan tʰištiʔin tʰaʔan k'umuy ʔan way'kʰat tiŋta yow ʔan hapas 13. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ witʔin šiʔčʰe·niʔin ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ to·yatiy 14. yow tʰawnaw šut'aʔhan'a šut'un sistiʔin ʔamaʔ tʰa·ŋi tʰihin' ha·ʔatʰaw kačʰiy 15. wɨxnat ʔinšiš tuytʰun ha·pʰuy
\xe 1. Mariposa Lily 2. Long ago they gathered almost everything for Indian food. 3. And they would dig the Mariposa Lily. 4. They would dig it with their digging stick. 5. When they dig a lot. 6. Then they will take it into the house putting it in their seive. 7. They rub it all to get off its dirt and skin, 8. And they boil it right away. 9. If they want it roasted then they don't peel it. 10. And they bury it in ashes. 11. And take it from the ashes when it's done. 12. And when it is done they take it out and clean off all the dirt and ashes. 13. And when it cools they eat it right away. 14. Also in the morning it is boiled, it will be boiled and when cooked it will be mixed with whatever they have. 15. It is good being eaten sweet.
\sfx media\mariposa lily story.mp3
\dt 21/Feb/2019

\lx k'išy'in
\ps n
\ge redbells
\sf media\red bells.mp3
\pc media\red bells picture.jpg
\sc Calandrinia ciliata
\nt picture from Wikipedia
\nt The seeds from this plant were used as a food and here is Cecile's account of collecting and preparing it:
\xv 0. k'išy'in 1. hawtaw k'išy'in čʰo·ŋačʰiŋ ʔan ʔitw'a 2. ʔamaʔ šimiyaʔ ʔan xo·ʔoxiʔ 3. ʔamaʔ maxa·ʔat tʰihin' tʰaŋ hutʰo·mat tʰihin' tʰaŋ 4. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ ʔot'yit hawaw tʰa ṭ'at'a hawšun tʰaw ʔopʰoʔtʰoʔ šiymanutʰa 5. šiyamunšu ʔan ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ wo·ṭʰowiṭʰ wič'e·tʰaŋ 6. ʔamaʔ ʔan k'umuy šiymiʔin konšu 7. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ way'kʰat ʔan-nim t'ap't'ap' woyit ʔan 8. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ witʔin t'aywaniw ʔot'o·yin 9. k'umuy ʔan way'kʰat ha·ʔa 10. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ witʔin mit'ač' pʰəwsat 11. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ ʔan pʰɨwɨsʔɨn ṭ'atʔin ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ witč'ʔiŋ ʔitk'aŋ ʔot'o·yin 12. təʔsat ye·t'aw tʰihin' tʰaŋ tʰaw t'o·mat'im 13. ʔamaʔ nay ha·ʔa təʔsat 14. ʔamaʔ hawtaw tʰihin' tuyhačʰinat ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ wat'a·xat hawit nay tʰihin' to·yatiy 15. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ kačʰyat t'epʰna yowwe·ʔi ha·ʔatʰaw
\xe 0. Redbells 1. When redbuds have finished their flowering. 2. And it has its seeds. 3. And they get it pulling it up. 4. Then they put it somewhere under a tree for however long it takes to dry. 5. When it's dried then they beat it with a stick. 6. Then all its dried {seeds} drop. 7. Then they take out its-my leaves and its whole plant. 8. Then right away they put it in the sifting basket. 9. All of its cleaned stuff. 10. Then right away they pound a little. 11. And they will put the {flour} resulting from the splitting and pounding in a small amount of water. 12. Making it gather together (like a cake). 13. And like making whatever. 14. And when they want to eat, then they break off however much they are eating. 15. Then they mix it with acorn mush or whatever they have.
\sfx media\red bells story.mp3
\mr nominative case
\nt Although referred to as "redbells" by consultant, this plant is also known in California as "red maids" 
\dt 26/Apr/2018

\lx mono·xič'
\ps n
\ge black root, Bracken Fern root
\sf media\black root.mp3
\nt Here is Cecile's description of preparing black root:
\xv 0. mono·xič' 1. monoxč'i maʔ tʰaʔan hopʰtat 2. ʔamaʔ maʔ tʰaŋ wɨxe·niʔin ṭ'oto·ʔot 3. ʔamaʔ maʔ tʰaŋ meṭʰa·hat ʔamaʔ maʔ tʰaŋ sa·kas ʔan šipešpat 4. ʔamaʔ maʔ tʰaŋ ṭ'ə·waṭ'iw xapʰtiw ʔitk'aw 5. ʔamaʔ maʔ tʰaŋ yow čoʔpat 6. (ʔamaʔ) tʰaŋ piyʔin tʰaŋ hoʔoy'owow ʔitk'aw ṭ'ə·waṭ'iw yet' tʰoyon'ow 7. ʔamaʔ maʔ tʰaŋ yow sa·kas ʔan šipešpat 8. tʰihin' tʰaŋ yow ṭ'ə·waṭ'iw ʔamaʔ tʰihin' yow šipešpat ʔan sa·kas 9. hawtaw tʰihin' tʰaŋ ṭ'ə·waṭ'iw hoʔoy'owow ʔitk'aw ham'at ṭɨmɨxwittʰa 10. hawtaw ʔan pʰaw'pʰuč' ṭɨmɨxwɨy ʔamaʔ tʰihin' šiymanutʰa 11. šiyamnunšiw ʔan tʰihin' tʰaŋ šototʰto·tʰa 12. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ ṭ'ə·waṭ'iw hawtaw tʰihin' ʔanik' ʔoho·ʔot tixta 13. ʔamaʔ hiyam ʔinšiš
\xe 0. Bracken Fern root 1. You also dig black root. 2. And when you get a bunch of them, peel them. 3. And you stretch them, scraping off the glue. 4. Then you soak it in hot water. 5. Then you also divide it. 6. finish it by soaking it in rusty water for one night. 7. Then you again scrape off its glue. 8.  They then again soak it and again scrape off its glue. 9. They soak it submerged in rusty water for however black (they want it to be). 10. Whenever it turns black, then they dry it. 11. When its dried, they coil it. 12. Then they soak however much they want to make a basket. 13. Then it is good.
\sfx media\Preparing black root story.mp3
\u mono·xič'/ -0
\mr nominative case
\dt 13/Jul/2018

\lx mono·xič'/
\ps n-theme
\ge black root, the tap root of Bracken Fern
\nom mono·xič'
\acc monoxč'i
\sc Pteridium aquilinum (western brackenfern)
\pc media\Bracken fern combined.jpg
\nt pictures from Wikipedia and Gamble
\ee Pictures show a Brackenfern plant and part of black root preparation process
\nt Brackenfern plant picture by Rasbak: wikimedia.org
\dt 21/Feb/2019

\lx mo·y'uk'/
\ps n-theme
\ge whirlwind
\loc mo·y'uk'iw
\nt Related language Yawelmani mo·y'ak'
\dt 02/Oct/2017

\lx mo·y'uk'iw
\ps n
\ge Whirlwind Place
\sf media\Whirlwind place.mp3
\xv ʔamaʔ tʰihik' ŋawši mo·y'uk'iw
\xe And they arrived at Whirlwind place.
\sfx media\And they arrived at Whirlwind place.mp3
\u mo·y'uk'/ iw
\mr locative case
\ee The name of a place of special doings. A hole was there where people ate dirt before they had hands--related to the beginning of the world story. Here is Cecile's telling of how people got their hands at Whirlwind Place:
\xv 0. mo·y'uk'iw 1. hiya·m'u may' pʰuṭʰoŋ ʔanik' nay čʰə·šašin 2. ʔam ʔanik' wastʰutʰaši ha·ʔa hawit kʰušne·lowaš yow we·ʔi tuya maxaʔ 3. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tiŋta tuyšu mo·y'uk'iw 4. ʔamaʔ tʰašti kʰay'iw ʔə·k'aš to·yatiy tʰihin' tiŋta 5. ʔamaʔ tošši limk'a tiŋta tʰihin' to·yatiy 6. ʔam wɨxnat ʔinšiš piye·na tʰihin' may'in p'aʔan'a wi tɨʔsɨn may' ʔanik' pʰuṭʰoŋ 7. ʔamaʔ tʰašti kʰay'iw tʰaŋ xa·xiy nim pʰuṭʰo·ŋa tʰašti wak'iy 8. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' hiʔ tuyo·na nay nim tuya 9. ʔamaʔ ṭ'uxit xa·xiy ʔam ʔinšiš ʔanik' pʰuṭʰo·ŋa witʔin xoʔen 10. ʔamaʔ k'ač'e·č'ay'w'o xa·xiy nim pʰuṭʰoŋ tʰašti wak'iy 11. ʔamaʔ tʰaŋ kʰay'iw tʰawṭʰuŋiṭʰ ʔoho·ʔoš 
\xe 0. Whirlwind Place 1. Long ago  people had hands like dogs' 2. They couldn't use their (hands) to cook or get food or anything. 3. And they ate dirt at Whirlwind place. 4. And coyote went to see them eating dirt. 5. Then he told Praire Falcon (Cecile changes this to Eagle later): They are eating dirt. 6. That is not good, they will eat up our land, we must make them hands. 7. Then coyote said to him: Give them my hands. 8. Then they will eat like I eat. 9. And Eagle said: It's no good, they couldn't live right with those hands. 10 Then lizard said: Give them my hands. 11. And coyote wanted to kill him right there. 
\sfx media\Whirlwind story 1.mp3
\xv 12. ʔamaʔ tiŋtaw k'ač'e·č'ay'w'o pʰuṭʰoŋ ʔan ʔot'oyši tiŋtaw 13. ʔamaʔ tʰaŋ kʰay'iw xay ʔiništʰaš tʰawṭʰuŋiṭʰ tʰaw ʔoho·ʔot 14. puč'ič' ʔamaʔ čʰitapʰ ʔan tawitʰ pʰatuwunšu yakʰawaw 15. ʔam tʰaŋ wastʰuš kʰay'win pʰičʰiw 16. ʔamaʔ hot'o·ʔoniš ʔamaʔ ʔitši tʰaŋ ʔoštʰo muṭʰuk'ta·tʰa tʰaŋ 17. ʔamaʔ tʰa·nitʰ tʰišši k'ač'e·č'ay'w'o niwit yow yakʰawaw waʔa hattinši 18. tʰa·nitʰ tʰaŋ kʰay'iwa hal'waštʰit 19. ṭ'uxit maxši ʔan tʰaŋ pʰuṭʰoŋin ʔan šə·pʰa tiŋtaw 20. ʔamaʔ tʰa·ŋi may'a wak'iyši pʰuṭʰo·ŋin tʰašti 21. tʰa·ŋi may' ʔinšiš ʔanik' pʰuṭʰoŋ 22. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' maxa·ʔat ʔanik' tuya šimiyaʔ k'umuy ha·ʔintʰa
\xe 12. Then lizard marked his hand in the dirt 13. And coyote jumped on him wanting to kill him. 14. Small and quick, he went into the rock. 15. The coyote couldn't catch him. 16. Then he built a fire and fanned the fire making it smoke. 17 Then lizard got out from there and climbed higher to another rock. 18. From there he was laughing at coyote. 19. Eagle got the hand (print) marked in the ground. 20 and he gave that to the people.  21. That's why people's hands are good. 22 And that's how they get their food, seeds, and anything else.
\sfx media\Whirlwind story 2.mp3

\nt Acquiring power through a dream helper was an important part of Wikchmani life. Here is Cecile's account of acquiring power: 
\xv 1. po·saʔ ʔanik' 2. hawtaw tʰihin' ʔaŋaṭʰwattʰa ʔiniša ha·ʔatʰaw ʔamaʔ hantʰaw tʰašti wittʰa ʔamaʔ tʰa ʔanik' po·saʔ 3. ʔamaʔ ʔipʰa·ʔat tʰihin' hiyam' šal'al'witaw 4. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' ʔam to·yatiy še·šatʰ yow tʰihin' hun'ay' tʰihin' ha·ʔatʰa ʔitaʔk'a to·yatiy hawšun tʰaw ʔopʰoʔtoʔ 5. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' may' wɨxnat patw'un ʔanik' tʰa po·saʔ ʔanik' pottʰaw 6. ʔamaʔ tʰaw tʰihin' čʰan'iʔ tuyo·na ha·ʔatʰa 7. amaʔ tʰihin' ha·ŋi tʰaw wita ʔanik' tʰa po·saʔ ʔaŋṭʰat ʔanik' 8. ʔamaʔ tʰaw tʰihin' maxa·ʔat ʔanik' tiy'm'ay'xay hiyam tʰašti hiyam ʔinešʔin ʔaŋaṭʰwat ʔanik' 9. ʔamaʔ čaʔaʔpa hun'ay' pʰisa·ʔat yow čaʔaʔpa hun'ay' ʔam čʰupo·nat haʔa 10. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' hun'ay' witʔin xo·ʔoxiy po·saʔ ʔan ʔam wɨxnat č'o·w'tʰo 11. han'tʰa ʔam pʰisa·ʔat yow hičʰa ʔaŋiṭʰwit tʰaŋ tɨʔɨsšat 12. ʔamaʔ tʰaw pʰisa·ʔat ṭʰaʔaṭʰi
\xe 1. Their Power 2. When they dream well, whatever the dream tells them, then that is their power. 3. Then they swim early in the morning. 4. And they can't eat meat but they eat anything that is watery for as many days as needed. 5. And they, people, do that a lot until their power will go into their body. 6. And then they will again eat something. 7. And whatever they dream they do by their power. 8. Then they get whatever power has been made good for them in their dream. 9. And some are at first told to doctor but some do not believe anything at first. 10. And if they go that way then their power does not work much. 11. If they do not want to doctor then their dream makes them. 12. Then they doctor people.
\sfx media\Acquiring power story.mp3

\lx pʰutʰsa
\ps n
\ge acorn
\sf media\acorn {acc}.mp3
\pc media\Valley oak combined.jpg
\nt Pictures from Sacramento State Online Natural History Museum csus.edu
\nt Acorns were a major food source for the Wikchamni. Here is Cecil'e account of gathering, preparing and eating acorns: 
\xv 1. hawtaw wosow ʔamaʔ tʰihin' ʔoho·ʔot ʔanik' hač'a·muʔ pʰutʰsa maxaʔ 2. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ tamna·tʰa ʔɨk'a·ʔat ʔan hiyam sisši 3. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' šut'o·mat ʔan kʰac'uč'uʔ 4. ʔamaʔ hičʰa hiyam k'iw'iš 5. hiyam ʔinšiš maxtʰin 6. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ wo·sowis tiŋtaw ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ nopo·ʔot 7. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' ha·ʔa t'am'uy'k'aw tʰihin' tʰaŋ nopo·ʔot yow ʔanik' hičʰa xo·ʔoxiʔ k'ač'awo ʔamaʔ tʰihin' k'ač'awiw tʰaŋ nopo·ʔot 8. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ šoŋo·tot tʰaw ʔanik' k'aṭʰu kawa·yuʔ 9. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' toʔṭ'ow ʔanik' šuŋo·tot tʰaŋ ṭʰe·ʔiw 10. ʔamaʔ tʰihin tʰoyon'ow tʰaw tʰihin' tʰaŋ ṭ'oto·ʔot 11. ʔamaʔ hičʰaʔ xo·ʔoxiʔ ʔɨtə·mɨč' ʔamaʔ ʔətmat minik' ṭ'otšiw toyon'ow 12. ʔamaʔ hawtaw naʔan maŋaščʰinat 13. ʔamaʔ hičʰa ʔam pe·yapiy ʔan ʔətma 14. ʔamaʔ tʰaw tʰaŋ tute·ʔat 15. ʔamaʔ ye·t'aw k'aw yow toyon'ow ʔamaʔ tʰaw nan yow tuse·ʔat ʔan ʔɨtəm 16. ʔamaʔ ʔan ṭ'otʔin pʰutʰsa k'umuy 17. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ šiymanutʰa šiyamunšiw ʔan ʔamaʔ tʰihin tʰaŋ ʔot'yit hawaw ʔanik' tʰaw tʰihin' tʰaŋ tute·ʔat 18. ʔamaʔ hičʰa tʰihin' tɨʔasčʰinat t'epʰna 19. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' maxa·ʔat 20. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' hokʰyat č'axšiyin pʰutʰsaŋ 21. č'axšiyin pʰutʰus ʔinšiš hac'a·m'uʔ tʰa·ŋi hokʰyaŋiṭʰ 22. ʔamaʔ ʔan ʔinišiyin watxutʰa ʔitik' 23. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ tʰa·muč' pʰəwsat 24. ʔamaʔ hawtaw tʰihin' ʔipna pʰaw'aʔa təʔsat pʰəwsat 25. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ yuk'o·ʔot 26. yuk'o·ʔot tʰihin' ʔan ṭʰumk'aŋ ʔaypʰun ʔitk'aŋ ʔot'yit 27. ʔamaʔ yow xapʰtu k'umuy ʔan k'ɨyɨṭʰɨn tʰiššiw 28. ʔamaʔ hiyam ʔan ʔipin ʔinšiš 29. amaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ t'epʰna·tʰa 30. t'epʰniʔin təʔsat xunay nahni tʰihin' tʰaŋ witaʔa ʔot'yit yow tʰihin' tʰaŋ təʔsat pisikʰč'a 31. pana tʰihin' təʔsat 32. xoto·wot tʰa han tʰa ʔanik' pan 33. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ č'ič'ik'ta·tʰa 34. ʔamaʔ hawtaw č'ič'e·k'atʰa ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ hiyam tuyšu 35. wɨxi ʔanik' tʰaŋ č'ow'hiy k'ač'awiw tʰihin' tʰaŋ ʔot'yit 36. hawtaw ʔitk'aw ʔaypʰun xotwoʔ ʔot'yit šə·ʔačʰinšiw ʔan 37. ʔamaʔ tʰihinʔ k'ač'awo ʔot'o·yin ṭʰe·ʔiw 38. ʔamaʔ tʰa hiyam ʔinšiš ʔanik' tuy 39. hawtaw tʰihin' tuyhačʰinat 40. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' t'uṭ'a yowwe·ʔi ha·ʔatʰa tʰaŋi tʰaŋ to·yatiy ye·tʰaw
\xe 1. When it's autumn then they want to get their fresh acorn. 2. And they try it looking to see if it is already ripe. 3. And they take off its acorn cap. 4. And it might already be hard. 5. It is ready to be taken. 6. Then they shake them to the ground and pick them up. 7. And whatever there is they gather into a strainer or if theirs is a basket, then they gather it into the basket. 8. And they carry it on their back if they have no horse. 9. Then they carry it home on their head. 10. And at night they peel them there. 11. And if they have a story teller then they tell stories while peeling at night. 12. And when we want to sleep. 13. And he isn't finishing telling his story. 14. Then he leaves it there. 15. And the next night he tells me his story again. 16. Then all the acorns get peeled. 17. Then they dry it, when it is dry they put it and leave it in whatever {container} they have. 18. And if they want to make acorn mush. 19. Then they get it. 20. Then they mix it with live oak acorn. 21. Fresh live oak acorn is good to mix in with it. 22. Then to be better, water is passed through it. 23. Then they pound it like that. 24. And when they have made enough flour by pounding. 25. Then they leach it. 26. They leach their {flour} by first putting warm water on it. 27. And again with hot water all of the bitterness comes out. 28. Then their acorn flour is good. 29. Then they make acorn mush. 30. Once the acorn mush is all done, they will put it like that or sometimes make bisquits. 31. They make bread. 32. They make hard acorn bisquits and that is their bread. 33. Then they make it cold. 34. And when it gets cold then they eat it. 35. They work a lot putting it into the basket. 36. They cool when the bisquits are first put into the water. 37. Then they put the basket in the house. 38. And it is good for their food. 39. When they want to eat. 40. Then they eat soup or whatever they have with it.
\sfx media\Gathering acorns 1-3.mp3
\u pʰutʰus/ -a
\mr accusative case
\dt 21/Feb/2019

\lx pʰutʰus

\ge acorn
\sc Quercus lobata
\pc media\Valley oak acorn 2.jpg
\nt Image By Parande at English Wikipedia, CC BY-SA 3.0, https://commons.wikimedia.org/w/index.php?curid=38184201
\sf media\acorn.mp3
\nt Here is Cecile's description of gathering and preparing ripe Valley Oak acorns:
\xv 0. This will be about the ripening of the acorn when it's turning black and they fall to the ground. 1. hawtaw pʰutʰus se·sasis ʔamaʔ kune·ʔat ṭ'at'a·nitʰ ṭɨmɨxwitʔin 2. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ konšiw ʔan k'umuy ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ nopo·ʔot 3. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ ṭʰe·ʔiw tʰantiʔin 4.  ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ wɨt'a·ʔat ʔopʰoʔtow hite·nitʰ šiyamun ʔinišiyin 5. ʔamaʔ tʰaw tʰihin' tʰaŋ tute·ʔat 6. hawtaw xot'e·ʔat ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ ṭʰo·moṭʰim 7. ʔamaʔ ʔan ṭ'əwnat 8. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' ʔopʰotšiw ʔan ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ yow mun'aw ʔot'yit 9. ʔamaʔ hawtaw k'umuy šaʔnat ʔamaʔ hiyam pʰaw'ši tʰaŋ pošwoʔo 10. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' maxa·ʔat ʔanik' k'ɨtšɨ sokʰotonat ṭʰipʰin 11. ʔamaʔ yow puč'ič' yakʰaw tʰa ʔanik' woṭʰo·hiy tʰa 12. ʔamaʔ tʰa·ŋi tʰihin' tʰaŋ ṭ'a·taṭ'it 13. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ ṭ'atʔin tʰišta·tʰa tʰa ʔanik' ha·nitʰ ṭ'oto·ʔot 14. ṭ'atʔin tʰihin' tʰaŋ ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ pʰaw'tinhat hawšun ʔanik' tʰaw ṭ'at 15. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ šiymanutʰa yow 16. ʔamaʔ ʔopʰoʔtoʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ yow ʔot'yit šiyamnunšiw ʔan ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ tʰantiʔin ṭʰe·ʔiw 17. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ ʔot'yit ʔanik' ʔutiy'iy'iw 18. hawtaw tʰihin' pʰɨwaščʰinat ʔamaʔ tʰihin' ṭ'o·pʰaṭ'ipʰ 19. hawšun tʰaw ʔanik' hawit nay ʔanik' tʰaw hiy'a tʰaŋ tɨʔsa ʔanik' ʔoho·ʔot ʔalinaʔ 20. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ pošwat 21. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ pʰəwsat 22. pʰɨwɨsʔin ʔamaʔ hawtaw tʰihin' t'epʰnaŋiṭʰ ʔoho·ʔot 23. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰayiŋin 24. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaw do·xatix tʰaŋ ʔan ʔipna 25. ʔamaʔ ʔipʰna ʔan tʰihin' ṭʰumk'aŋ ʔitk'aŋ ʔaypʰun ʔot'yit 26. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ ʔan tʰaw ṭ'iwɨnšɨ 27. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' xapʰtiw ʔot'o·yin tʰaw ʔamaʔ ʔinišiyin tʰe·ʔatʰiš ʔitik' 28. ʔam tʰihin tʰaŋ hokʰyat č'axšiyin pʰutʰsaŋ meʔči hiyam sisaʔaʔ ʔinišiyin ʔitik' watxutʰa 29. hač'a·m'u tʰihin' tʰaŋ hokʰyat meʔči ʔam wɨxnat sisaʔaʔ ʔamaʔ ʔam wastʰutʰa ʔitk'in watxuŋiṭʰ
\xe 0. This will be about the ripening of the acorn when it's turning black and they fall to the ground. 1.When the acorns ripen, turn black, and fall from the tree. 2. When they all fall, then they pick them up. 3. And they will take it to the house. 4. Then they spread it out in the sun where it will dry good 5. They leave it up there. 6. When it rains then they cover it up. 7. Then it doesn't get wet. 8. Then they put it outside in the sun again. 9. And when it is all loosened up then it is ready to crack. 10. They get their soaprock with holes on the top. 11. And also a small rock that is their hitter. 12. And that is what they crack with. 13. And as a result of their cracking and peeling they take it out of there. 14. As a result they get enough of how ever much they crack there. 15. Then they dry it again. 16. Then they put it in the sun again, when dried they take it into the house. 17. And they put it into their container. 18. When they want to pound then they get ground acorns. 19. Later they get however much they want to make their flour. 20. Then they crack it. 21. And they pound it. 22. As a result of the pounding, when they want acorn mush to be made. 23. Then they make a leaching hole. 24. Then they pour their flour in it. 25. Then they first put warm water on their acorn flour. 26. Then it is soaked there. 27. And they will put hot water there and it comes out as good water. 28. They don't mix it with live oak acorn because it is ripe enough for water to pass through well. 29. The new ones they have to mix because they are not ripe enough and the water is unable to pass through.

\nt The following story is Cecile's version of the Beginning of the World:
\xv 1. hiyam'u xew ʔə·k'at ʔitik' 2.ʔaṭʰaʔtʰa p'aʔan'a ʔamaʔ tʰan puč'ič' ʔam may'a ṭ'at'e·ʔaw 3. ʔamaʔ xew tʰihin' xo·ʔoxiʔ 4. ʔamaʔ xew xoʔoxʔoš ṭ'uxit yow kʰay'iw 5. ʔamaʔ sawaxitʰ tʰaštiw tʰanši. 6. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ maxta·tʰa wak' hinwit ʔatita ŋawit 7. ʔamaʔ hiyam tʰawaṭʰčʰinat ʔamaʔ hawaʔčay ʔan kʰe·šikʰ tʰaw wa·k'aš ʔakayč'i 8. ʔamaʔ xuyiwši k'umuy ʔə·k'atʰin yow p'aʔan' k'amoʔotʰa 9. ʔamaʔ kʰay'iw ʔə·k'aš ʔan kʰe·šikʰaw tʰaw wak'tʰin ʔamaʔ puk'ši tʰaw wa·k'aš 10. ʔamaʔ ṭ'uxit tʰaŋ maxši ʔamaʔ tʰaŋ ʔot'oyšiw 11. ʔamaʔ maxši ha·ʔa ʔamaʔ tʰaŋ hukuyšu wakʰʔaš xiŋ ha·ŋi šomwitaʔan 12.  ʔamaʔ tʰaw tʰaʔan teʔisšu may'a ʔamaʔ č'utipʰiy kʰuhṭ'un ʔamaʔ č'utipʰiy muk'ešhin
\xe 1. Long ago only water was seen there. 2. A small piece of land was opened but there were no people or trees there. 3. And they are living there. 4. Eagle and Coyote were living there. 5. And turtle went to them all. 6. And they sent him to get whatever he could when he got to the bottom. 7. And he wanted to die and he barely got his portion under his fingernails by pulling it from a deep place. 8. Then he returned and all the dirt he had seen was gone. 9. And Coyote looked at all his fingernails to get something there and got a grain of dirt there 10. And Eagle took it and put it aside. 11. And he took whatever of value and mixed this with something else of value. 12. And he also turned some of that there into people, six men and six women.
\sfx media\begin world part 1.mp3
\xv 13. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' puŋpiŋy'iʔin tʰanši xoʔe niwatwašaw hitewtʰin xoʔoʔ 14. ʔamaʔ tʰa ṭ'uxit tʰanta xoyiš kʰay'wa ʔə·k'aš tʰašti ṭʰaʔaṭʰi hawaʔtʰaʔ tʰihin' 15. kʰay'iw xuyiwši ʔamaʔ xa·xiy ʔam tʰihin' ʔinišiyin xo·ʔoxiʔ to·yatiy tʰihin' tiŋta min hin čuʔupan'a tiŋit k'aṭʰutʰa 16. ʔamaʔ ṭ'uxit xa·xiy wɨxnat ʔam ʔinšiš 17. puk'e·na ha·ʔa ʔa·mat tɨʔɨsɨn tʰaŋ ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tuyo·na 18. ho·yihiy we·ʔi ʔupʰyeyyiw hin hiʔ ha·ʔa puk'e·na 19. ʔamaʔ tʰa tʰanši ʔupʰyeyyiʔ ʔamaʔ puk'ši šimiyaʔ 20. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tɨʔsɨn tʰa·nitʰ tuya 21.  ʔamaʔ ṭ'uxit yow kʰay'iw ʔot'oyši tʰaŋ tiŋtaw 22. ʔamaʔ tʰaw p'aʔn'aw woyšu k'umuy hantʰaw 23. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' ṭʰaʔaṭʰi tošši tuy ʔan taŋtʰam tʰašti 24. ʔamaʔ hawtaw šimiyaʔ šiymana yow hantʰaw hisišaʔa ʔamaʔ ṭʰaʔaṭʰi tʰaŋ maxši 25. ʔamaʔ xi ṭʰaʔaṭʰi wɨxe·ʔaš yow k'umuy ʔanik' yuw'k'e·ʔaš ʔamaʔ tʰa ʔitik' hetʰtʰa xo·ʔoxiʔ tʰaw p'aʔan'a
\xe 13. And they went two at a time, living at different places that is where they will be. 14. And the Eagle sent Coyote who attempted and saw how all those people were doing. 15. Coyote returned and said they are not doing well, why are they are eating your dirt, half of the dirt is gone. 16. And Eagle said this is really not good. 17. We will find something to help make it then they will be able to eat. 18. Calling to dove, will you be able to find anythingʔ 19. And the dove went and found seeds. 20. And they could make food from that. 21. Then Eagle and Coyote put them in the ground. 22. And there in the ground everything was born. 23. And they told the people eat all of your food 24. And when the seeds were dry and whatever was hidden then they got them. 25. And these people increased and all of them went to their homes and the water is still there under the land.

\lx šaxathay
\ps n
\de loose woven, twined basket
\sf media\loosely woven, twined.mp3
\nt Here is Cecile's discussion of twined baskets:
\xv 1. šaxathay ʔam wɨxnat ʔač'ew tixaʔtan'a 2. ʔamaʔ ʔam pʰečʰwat ʔitk'a 3. yow hičʰa maʔ ʔoho·ʔot min ʔitk'a ʔot'oy tʰaw tuya min 4. ʔamaʔ maʔ tʰaŋ mit'ačʰ ʔač'ew xa·t'axit' 5. ʔamaʔ pʰečʰwat ʔitk'a 6. čaʔaʔpa nahni hun'ay' šaxathay təʔsat ʔan ʔot'yit xi ʔanik' pana yow we·ʔi ha·ʔatʰa ʔanik' tuya 7.  ho· ʔanik' t'epʰnin k'ač'aw ʔamaʔ wɨxnat ʔač'ew tixaʔtan'a ʔamaʔ yow huč'ayʔiy 8. ʔamaʔ ʔinišiyin pʰečʰwat ʔitk'a
\xe 1. A twined basket is not very closely woven. 2. It doesn't hold water. 3. Or if you want to put water there with your food. 4. Then you stitch it slightly closer. 5. Then it holds water. 6. At first some baskets are sometimes made loosely woven to put their bread or any of their food whatever. 7. To be ok for their acorn basket, then they weave very closely and also tightly. 8. It is good to hold water.
\sfx media\Twined basket story.mp3
\nt Cecile's basket to the left of this picture is a good example of the loosely woven twined technique.
\pc media\Twined basket.jpg
\nt image from Gamble
\dt 22/Feb/2019

\lx taŋay
\ps n
\ge Jimsonweed
\sf media\jimsonweed.mp3
\nt Although it is a poisonous and dangerous plant, Jimsonweed was used to induce dreams for acquiring power, in a ritualized way to speed recovery from childbirth, and as a medicinal plant. Here is Cecile's account of Jimsonweed use:
\xv 0. taŋay 1. hiyamxay taŋay ʔokʰnat 2. tʰa·ŋi tʰihin' t'oyoxwišat 3. tʰaŋ tʰihin' ʔukunʔun taŋayin 4. yet' may' ʔuko·n'utʰa tʰa·ŋi watʰaw ʔoho·ʔot ʔan taŋyiwšu 5. hawtaw tʰihin' ʔoknat ʔamaʔ tʰihin' taŋayin we·ʔi hawšuntʰaw ʔopʰoʔtoʔ 6. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' taʔatat ʔukonŋiṭʰ taʔatat 7. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' yow taŋy'in'in ʔɨk'a·ʔat čʰɨxačʰnič'a 8. yow čaʔaʔpa ʔokʰnat tʰaŋ ʔamaʔ ṭʰipʰenna·tʰa 9. yow ʔan t'ap't'ap' ʔinšiš hawtaw maʔ ṭʰaṭʰyit min č'iy'a 10. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ ʔot'yit ṭʰaṭʰayiw 11. ʔamaʔ tʰaŋ taʔa·titʰa 12.  yow ʔinšiš tapʰičʰnaw 13. ʔamaʔ yow ʔan ho·pʰut ʔaxitnač'i 14. maxa·ʔat ʔan ho·pʰut 15. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ sokʰtat hawtaw ʔanik' puč'ič' ʔa·xit hač'amway'o 16. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' k'iw'ši tuyhačʰinat 17. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' wamičʰin tʰaw tʰuhna·tʰa 18. ʔamaʔ ʔam witʰe·pa čʰɨxa·čʰanti
\xe 0. Jimsonweed 1. Old time people drank Jimsonweed. 2. They doctored themselves with it. 3. They would get dizzy drinking it. 4. One person made whoever wants to get dizzy drink it. 5. When they drank it they would get dizzy for so many days. 6. Then they get over the dizziness, the ones who would be drunk by it get well. 7. And the ones who get dizzy also see those who are sick. 8. And some drink it and get power. 9. Also its leaves are good when you have a broken bone. 10. And they put it on the broken place. 11. And it makes it well. 12. It is also good for arthritis. 13. And its roots are also [used] by one who has had a baby. 14. They get its roots. 15. And they make a hole in it when their little one is just born. 16. And when they want to eat hard [food]. 17. And they will chew [some of the desired food] and spit it into [the hole]. 18. Then the baby doesn't get sick.
\sfx media\Jimsonweed story.mp3
\pc media\Jimsonweed combined.jpg
\nt Pictures by Gamble and Steven Mark Norris, CSUCI
\sc Datura wrightii, Datura meteloides
\u taŋay/ -0
\mr nominative case
\dt 22/Feb/2019

\lx tʰakʰaʔkʰaʔ
\ps n
\ge Redbud
\sf media\redbud.mp3
\xv tʰakʰaʔkʰaʔ tʰihin' ʔana·pʰat
\xe They are splitting Redbud.
\sfx media\They are splitting Redbud.mp3
\pc media\Redbud.jpg
\nt pictures from Gamble
\nt Cecile collecting Redbud for baskets and a close up of the Redbud stems
\nt Here is Cecile's account of collecting and preparing Redbud for baskets:
\xv 0. tʰakaʔkʰaʔ 1. maxa·ʔat tʰihin' tʰakʰaʔkʰaʔ 2. puyuŋšiw ʔan tʰa tʰaŋ k'iw'išta·tʰa 3. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' ma·xin tʰaŋ wat'a·xat 4. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ takta·tʰa yet' tʰoyon'ow 5. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ yow watʰa·xat ʔan hoŋhoŋ 6.  hawtaw ʔiništʰa tixatčʰinat tʰihin' 7. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ yow hɨpʰa·ʔat hiyam ʔinšiš ʔanik' tʰaʔan tixittʰin 8. ʔam ʔinšiš teyaw puyoŋ maxtʰin 9. k'iw'išta·tʰa tʰaŋ puyoŋ 10.  xoʔ ʔan ṭ'ayax tʰaŋ tʰihin' way'kʰat hawtaw tʰihin' tixatčʰinat 11. hawtaw tʰihin' ʔoho·ʔot hawit ʔanik' tʰaw tixtin tʰa·nitʰ ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaw tʰaŋ
\xe 0. Redbud 1. They get Redbud. 2. After it frosts it makes it hard. 3. And they will take it and split it. 4. Then they make it stay over night. 5. Then they again split it's heart-wood. 6. When it is ready, they want to weave a basket. 7. Then they also scrape it and it is good to also be woven by them. 8. It is not good to get before it frosts. 9. The frost makes it hard. 10. If there are knobs where new growth comes, they clean them off when they want to weave a basket. 11. When they want to use it, they will weave away from that spot.
\sfx media\Preparing Redbud story.mp3
\cf tʰukʰuta 'redbud tree'
\dt 22/Feb/2019

\lx tʰakʰa·či

\ge palm of hand
\dt 15/Oct/2017

\lx tʰakʰa·tʰiʔ
\ps n
\ge sourberry
\sf media\sourberry {nom}.mp3
\sc Rhus trilobata (aromatica)
\pc media\sourberry.jpg
\nt Image from commons.wikimedia.org
\nt The berries of this plant are very sour but often eaten. Here is Cecile's account of sourberry:
\xv 0. tʰakʰa·tʰiʔ 1. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ hayatšiw sisšiw ʔan maxa·ʔat šuy'uk' 2. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' ʔan ma·xin ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ tʰantiʔin ʔanik' pʰə·wasiw mit'ač' pəwsat 3. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ witʔin ʔitk'aŋ hokʰyat 4. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ to·yatiy tʰaw witʔin 5. nahni tʰihin' ha·ʔatʰaw č'it'at' to·yatiy tʰa·ŋi ye·t'aw yow we·ʔi hučen'aw yič'ŋa tʰaŋ to·yatiy 6. ʔamaʔ wɨxnat šuy'uk' nahni xoʔo·ši yow ʔanik' hičʰa xo·ʔoxiʔ hantʰaw hapʰuy 7. ʔamaʔ tʰa·ŋi tʰihin' tʰaŋ hokʰyat ʔamaʔ hapʰoya·tʰa 8. k'aṭʰu ʔanik' tʰa ʔašukaʔ hiya·m'u 9. xoʔo·ši nimik' ʔašukiŋ ʔamaʔ naʔan hukuyʔin tʰaŋ ʔašukaŋ to·yatiy yow čaʔaʔpa tʰaŋ kʰuyoŋ hokʰyat 10. wɨxnat tʰaʔan ʔinšiš wɨxi tʰaŋ wɨxnat hetʰtʰa maxa·ʔat
\xe 0. Sourberry 1. When summer comes and when it ripens, then they get it sour. 2. They will get it and take it to their mortar and pound it slightly. 3. Then they mix it with water right away. 4. And they eat it right away like that. 5. Sometimes they eat it with clover ot whatever or they eat it like that alone. 6. And it is very sour, sometimes if they have something sweet 7. Then they mix it with that and it sweetens it. 8. They didn't have sugar long ago. 9. When we have some sugar then we mix it with that and eat it or mix it with some salt. 10. It is also very good so many still get it.
\sfx media\Sourberry story.mp3
\mr nominative case
\nt Related language Chukchansi tʰaxa·tʰiʔ (NAS)
\dt 17/Apr/2018

\lx ṭʰišaw
\ps n
\ge homeplace, how consultant refers to her chilhood home
\sf media\homeplace.mp3
\nt Here is Cecile's story of growing up at her homeplace:
\xv 0. ṭʰišaw 1. waʔ nimik' p'aʔan' xoʔoxʔoš ṭʰišaw 2. tʰa·nitʰ nan pohuṭʰšu no·nipʰ naʔan yuʔut witʰiphač' 3. nipʰeč' nimik' tʰa·wiṭʰši teyaw ʔan p'aʔan'a 4. ʔama naʔan mun'oš yič'ŋa pohuṭ'šu 5. pohuṭʰšu naʔan hatxin'in tʰanaʔat mo·y'uk'iw ʔipʰa·ʔat naʔan tʰaw ʔitk'aw 6. nahni nan tʰanaʔat ʔunaw ʔipʰe·ʔič' 7. ʔamaʔ našo·šun nimik' ʔuy'uʔutʰ p'aʔan' ṭʰišaw 8. ʔam yuʔut hiya·m'u šeʔokahan'a k'umuy tʰaw waʔ tʰanaʔat 9. našuš nimik' maxa·ʔat šimiyaʔ wat'tin'in 10. ʔamaʔ yow pʰutʰsa maxa·ʔat tʰa nimik' hiya·m'u tuy 11. yow hawtaw haya·tiw natʰetʰ nimik' ʔam č'ow'hiy k'ačʰn'ay'ay ṭʰukʰo·ʔat topʰṭʰa 12. ʔamaʔ našuš nimik' wɨxi ʔan ṭʰokʰšiw šiymanutʰa tʰa tomoxšiw nimik' tuyo·huy 13. yet' tʰišamyiw m'am'n'a t'oyonat nahni tʰaŋ tʰaʔan šiymanutʰa yow nahni tʰaŋ ṭʰoŋa·ṭʰiw 14. tʰomoxšiw nimik' ʔam wastʰutʰa tʰanŋiṭʰ suyanhanŋeṭʰa wɨxnat ʔan xot'šiw witʔin nimik' našuš ṭʰoŋa·ṭʰin yow šiymanuṭʰa nimik' tuyo·huy tʰomoxšiw 15. hawtaw nan k'umuy yiw'ič'naš ʔamaʔ nan ṭʰiššiw nimik' tʰaw xoʔen yič'ŋa nimik' pahathay tʰaw xoʔo·xiʔ 16. natʰetʰ nimik' huyoʔč'a kawa·yuʔ ʔamaʔ yow pa·kʰa yow haya·tiw č'o·w'otʰo
\xe 0. Homeplace 1.Our far land was homeplace. 2. Nine of us children grew up from there. 3. Our older brother died before his first year. 4. Then we eight grew up alone. 5. We grew up riding, going to Whirlwind Place swiming in the water there. 6. Sometimes we went swimming at Sand Flat. 7. And our mother used to own the land at the homeplace. 8. Long ago it wasn't fenced, everyone went to that place. 9. Our mother would get seeds of wild plants, 10. and also get acorns, that was our food long ago. 11. Also in summer when our father wasn't working, we would be fishing, catching fish 12. Then our mother would dry most of his catch, that was our winter food. 13. One spring (we would be) picking blackberries, sometimes drying them and sometimes canning them.14. In winter we couldn't go the store much because of the rain, that is why our mother canned and dried our winter food supply. 15. When we all got married then we left our living place and our parents lived there alone. 16. Our father liked horses and also cattle and worked in the summertime
\sfx media\homeplace story.mp3
\dt 24/Oct/2018

\lx ʔaŋṭʰuw
\ps n
\ge doctor
\sf media\doctor {nom}.mp3
\nt One of traditional ways of doctoring (pʰe·sa) included the doctor dancing for the patient. Here is Cecile's account of Doctor Dancing:
\xv 0. ʔaŋṭʰuw kʰam'a·ʔač' 1. kʰam'a·ʔač' xošošit ŋeʔwaw teyaw ʔan kʰam'a ʔɨtkʰač'i ʔan tʰaŋ xo·šoxiš 2. ʔamaʔ šiʔčʰin čʰan'iʔ kʰam'e·na meṭʰ tʰa·nitʰ ʔušitʰ hot'o·ʔoniš 3. ʔamaʔ ʔɨtkʰač'i ʔan tʰaštin ʔan ʔɨtkʰat ʔo·mo ʔan ʔuyyu ʔɨtkʰat ʔan ʔɨtkʰa wɨxi ṭʰaʔaṭʰi ʔoʔčiʔič' k'unimʔin ʔoštʰo xo·ʔoxiʔ 4. yet' muk'iš tʰaŋ ye·t'aw wuwo·tat tʰaŋ ʔɨtakʰič' muk'iš 5. ʔamaʔ ṭʰaʔaṭʰi maxa·ʔat ʔoʔče·ʔič'a kʰam'ta·tʰa tʰašti wak'yaŋiṭʰ kʰə·xaŋ 6. ʔamaʔ kʰa·m'akʰim' muk'e·ši yow kʰuhuṭ'hat kʰa·m'akʰim' tʰoyon'ow waʔ kʰam'a wɨxnat šal'al'wita 7. ʔamaʔ čʰuŋo·huy ʔan ʔɨtkʰa ʔɨtkʰat teyaw ʔopʰotiyin tʰašik' tʰaʔan čʰuŋo·tat 8. pʰuṭʰo·ŋaw ʔan wačam' ṭ'ow'oʔon'a wačam' ʔan tɨʔasan'a t'ič'pʰin p'a·tin yow ʔan ǯux toʔṭ'ow ʔot'oywišat 9. ʔamaʔ ʔan waliṭʰ ṭʰutṭʰuṭʰŋi tɨʔasan'a 10. ʔamaʔ ʔan yow čʰuŋiš ʔot'oywišat tʰa·ŋi 11. nahni pʰisa·ʔat čʰɨxɨčʰina ʔan kʰam'a tʰoyon'ow tʰihin tuyta·tʰa ṭʰaʔaṭʰi yow kʰo·pʰi
\xe 0. Doctor Dancer 1. The dancer painted at evening before his dancing, singers did the painting. 2. It will cool soon away from the big bonfire and they will dance. 3. Then the singers sing their song, when the starter starts the singing a lot of people sit around the bonfire and watch. 4. One of the women singers stands up with him. 5. The people get the watchers to dance by giving them money. 6. Then the women and men make a dance, dancing at night long until early morning. 7. And they finish singing their last song before the sun comes up. 8. He has a doctoring wand in his hand, his wand is made of a bird feather and his headress in put on his head. 9. And his necklace is made with abalone shells. 10. And he also has his apron on with it. 11. Sometimes his dancing at night is for a sick person, they feed the people including coffee.
\sfx media\doctor dancer story.mp3
\u ʔaŋṭʰuw/ -0
\mr nominative case
\va ʔaŋṭʰaw
\dt 07/Mar/2018

\lx ʔaxaʔčʰi
\ps n
\ge cradle
\sf media\cradle.mp3
\xv we·č'iyip' ʔaxaʔčʰi
\xe forked cradle board made of oak sapling
\sfx media\cradle made of oak.mp3
\nt Here is a picture of Cecile's cradleboard:
\pc media\Ceciles cradleboard and stirring stick.jpg
\nt picture from Gamble
\nt Here is Cecile's discussion of cradleboards and raising infants:
\xv 1. ʔo·mo witʰe·pin woyšiw ṭ'e·k'aṭ'k'aŋiṭʰ we·č'ip'in we·č'iŋ tɨʔasan' ʔaxaʔčʰi 2. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' ʔatit ʔot'yit wič'etʰ ṭ'e·k'aṭ'k'aŋiṭʰ 3. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ ṭ'ik'in ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaw ṭ'ek'aṭʰk'aŋiṭʰ witʰiphač' 4. maywe·ʔi hawtaw puhuṭʰ tʰaŋ ʔan ʔaxiʔčʰi ʔamaʔ tɨʔɨsaŋiṭʰ meṭʰ ʔaxiʔčʰi 5. tɨʔasan'a hičʰa we·ʔi šata·min wič'itʰhat tʰa·ŋi tɨʔɨsan'a yow ho·pʰutŋu 6. ʔamaʔ tʰaw tʰihin' ʔan ṭ'e·k'aṭ'ik' we·ʔi ʔan yet' yow poŋya ʔan ʔopʰšiw 7. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ muk'ešhin niwti ʔočoy yow kʰuhuṭ'ha·yin tʰaʔin tʰaŋ niwti ʔočoy 8. tʰaŋ tʰaw witaʔaw k'ənwat 9. ʔamaʔ tʰa tʰašti tʰanaštʰat waʔ kʰaʔama ʔanik' hiwe·tʰaw 10. tʰaw tʰihin' ṭ'e·k'aṭ'k'aŋiṭʰ hiyam' ʔanik' teyaw ʔanik' maxaʔ xiŋ ha·ʔa milikʰaniʔin mononhay 11.  ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tɨʔɨsšat tʰiwin yowwe·ʔi toppolin č'utya
\xe 1. When a child is first born they were tied in a cradleboard made of a forked stick of an oak sapling. 2. And they put down sticks when they were tied. 3. That is how they are tied and they tie the babies on there. 4. Sometimes when the baby gets to old for the cradle a big cradle is made. 5. They can be made with anything but they are mostly made with willow limbs or roots. 6. And they tie its [hands] there for one or two months. 7. And they had a different women's design and a different mens' design. 8. That is where they could be sleeping at anytime. 9. And that lasts them until they are walking. 10. They were tied like that long before they were taking white person's clothes. 11. And they made it from cottontail or sometimes jackrabbit skin.
\sfx media\Cradleboard story part 1.mp3
\xv 12. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰa·ŋi witʰiphat ṭ'e·k'aṭ'ik' 13. ʔamaʔ wičʰeyŋiṭʰ tʰe ʔaxaʔčʰi xoʔ ʔanik' wičʰeyŋi tʰa 14. ʔamaʔ tʰaw kʰuhuṭ'ha·yin tʰatupʰ yow sikʰetʰ wičʰeyiʔhan'a yow muk'ešhin hun'ay' xupʰa·ŋi tɨʔasan'a ʔanik' ʔaxiʔčʰi 15. ʔamaʔ ʔanik' k'ip'ew' tɨʔasan'a pʰo·taŋi 16. ʔamaʔ yow hohoŋin ʔiyluʔ tʰa ʔanik' k'ip'ew' nahni tʰihin' pʰo·tayŋi ʔoče·yitʰa 17. ʔamaʔ tʰa ʔaxiʔčʰi xew ṭʰipʰin ʔaniyč'iw ʔan ʔot'yiŋiṭʰ 18. ʔamaʔ tʰaŋ witʰiphat to·ʔatiʔ mitʰis pohoṭʰʔun tʰaw tʰihin' hun'ay' tute·ʔaŋiṭʰ 19. ʔamaʔ hawtaw tʰihin' č'o·w'otʰo 20. ʔamaʔ tʰihin' tʰaŋ toʔṭ'ow ʔanik' ʔot'oy k'iwe·tʰaw ʔan 21. ʔamaʔ tʰaw tʰihin' witʔin č'o·w'otʰo ṭ'uw'e·na ʔanik' ʔaxta ʔam tʰihin' tʰaŋ ṭʰe·ʔiw ʔot'yit 22. ho·wiʔ mine·ṭ'it witʰiphat nahni yet' p'aʔan'a poŋoy p'aʔan'a yow nahni čʰawpʰaw yit'šiŋta ʔanik' tʰaŋ tuytaŋiṭʰ ʔanik' tʰaw witʔin hiyam to·yatiy ʔamaʔ hetʰtʰa yow mine·ṭ'it
\xe 12. And they tied the children with it. 13. And those cradleboards will be decorated -- that is their design. 14. And the men's cradleboard was decorated with bow and arrow there and the moen's were made with different willow limbs. 15. And they make the cradle strap from beads. 16. And they also use milkweed string for their cradle strap sometimes they are decorated with beads. 17. And the ring around the top of the cradleboard is decorated that way. 18. And that is what the babies play with until they get older when they are first untied. 19. And when they work, 20. Then they put the baby on their head on it's back. 21. And while they work they will take their baby, they don't leave them at home. 22. Yes, they nurse their babies sometimes one year, two years, or sometimes continuously for five years they feed them even if the are already eating they still nurse them.
\sfx media\Cradleboard story part 2.mp3
\u ʔaxaʔčʰi/ -0
\mr nominative case
\dt 22/Feb/2019

\lx ʔoyyat
\ps v
\ge starting a basket
\sf media\start {d-pres}.mp3
\pc media\starting a basket combined.jpg
\nt pictures from Gamble
\ee beginning the initial coil and the first coils beginning a new basket
\nt Here is Cecile telling how she makes the basket start:
\xv 1. ʔoyyat k'ač'aw ho·pʰut šo·pʰin waʔa (max) 2. ʔamaʔ č'atik' toynow 3. ʔamaʔ yow max ho·pʰut pʰetʰpʰitʰ ṭʰipʰin xiŋ šo·pʰin ho·pʰut 4. hawit nay maʔ ʔoho·ʔot ʔan tɨʔsa miṭʰaʔa 5. ʔamaʔ maʔ tʰaŋ ŋəʔwɨn 5. ʔamaʔ maʔ tʰaŋ tixtin 6. ʔamaʔ maʔ ho·pʰutŋu tʰaŋ č'akšiyin 7. nay maʔ tʰaŋ hiʔ miṭʰe·ʔat teyaw min ʔot'oy č'akša 8. hawtaw maʔ č'akša ʔot'yit ʔamaʔ hiyam'a miṭʰe·ʔatʰatʰ
\xe 1. When starting a basket, (take) three long roots 2. then bend them in the middle. 3. Then get another root and wind it above the three roots. 4. However big you want to make it. 5. Then you bend it around. 6. And you will weave it. 7. You use the root as straw grass, 8. like when you make it bigger before you put your straw grass in. 9. When you put the straw grass in it is already been made bigger.
\sfx media\Starting a basket.mp3
\dt 22/Feb/2019

\lx ʔo·šaʔ
\ps n
\ge bottleneck basket
\sf media\bottleneck basket.mp3
\nt Here is Cecile's discussion about a bottleneck basket she was making:
\xv 1. təʔsat naʔ k'ač'aw 2. tʰišamyiw nim ʔuya·yitʰ 3. ʔamaʔ naʔ ŋa·waw ʔopʰoʔtow k'ənwat nahni yit'an yow we·ʔi puŋy'it 4. yow nahni čuʔupan'a nahni ʔam waʔa tʰaŋ č'o·w'otʰo 5. ʔamaʔ naʔ ʔuče·yišta tʰaŋ ṭʰəʔətɨn ʔočay 6. hiyam naʔ ʔam ʔo·šaʔ təʔsaš 7. ʔamaʔ tʰaŋ xiŋ tamna·tʰa ʔošaʔ naʔ tʰaŋ təʔsat 8. hinwitʔin pʰaw'e·na ṭɨmk'ɨtʰɨn mono·xič' 9. pe·nat nim mono·xič' yow haw'ečey' ho·pʰut yow maxa·ʔat
\xe 1. I am making a basket. 2. I started it in the springtime. 3. And I sometimes put down one or two rows a day. 4. or sometimes a half row or sometimes I don't work on it long. 5. And I am decorating it with the rattlesnake design. 6. I haven't made a bottleneck basket recently. 7. And I am trying to make this, to make a bottleneck basket. 8. I am not sure I have enough black, black root. 9. My black root is low and it is hard to get those roots.
\sfx media\Making a bottleneck basket story.mp3
\nt Here are a couple of Cecile's Bottleneck baskets:
\pc media\Bottleneck baskets (1).jpg
\nt pictures from Gamble
\nt also called a money basket
\nt Related language Chukchansi ʔo·ṣaʔ
\va ʔošaʔ
\dt 22/Feb/2019
